Translations

Five-Two-Five Flying Star New Car Wins Gold Cup 231

— Ying

According to news from 者靈, the Five-Two-Five Flying Star new car has won a gold cup awarded by the Persian Automobile Association, of considerable value. It is the most valuable prize awarded by the association in all its competitions since its establishment. The Persian National Automobile Competition, organized by the Automobile Association, commenced on June 22nd and lasted for eight days until June 29th. The competition consisted of eight events, all difficult journeys, covering a total distance of 3,100 kilometers, equivalent to 1,926 miles. The competition generated unprecedented excitement, attracting great attention from people all over Europe. Many individuals participated in the competition, with almost every car manufacturer from around the world participating. The competition rules were particularly strict, not only emphasizing the speed of travel and the durability of the components, but also including a two-kilometer speed test and a three-kilometer hill climb. Braking and acceleration were also tested in separate segments. All vehicles had to complete all the competition events to be eligible to win. It is evident that the hope of winning the championship was exceedingly difficult. During the race, there were strict secret parking regulations, and water was not allowed to be added to the radiator during the race. Under these circumstances, the Five-Two-Five Flying Star factory car "5252S" GiatTourere driven by Rahnenfeld was able to win the championship, ranking first. All the famous cars from various manufacturers were defeated, and its excellent performance was truly astonishing. It is said that the price of this new car ranges from 1,500 to 3,000 gold yuan, which is not expensive. At the same time, Rahnenfeld, the driver, also won the 1930 Persian Automobile Association's National Competition Medal.

Simplify Traffic Lights and Street Signs 232

— Jiren

Traffic lights installed at intersections are convenient for traffic and ensure public safety. To be effective, the traffic lights must be clearly visible and the signs must be simple, easy to distinguish, and understand. The American Engineering Bureau recently investigated the conditions of street signs, traffic lights, and markings in over one hundred large cities (each with a population of approximately 50,000). The following is a brief summary based on a report from the news agency of the American General Motors Manufacturing Plant.

Accurate markings are of great help to drivers, and are even more important for traffic management in bustling cities. Street numbers should be simple and their location should be conspicuous. For example, the wording should be clear, the colors should be standard, the format should be consistent, and the location should be appropriate. All of these factors must be carefully considered. Unauthorized posters and miscellaneous signs should be prohibited and removed from intersections.

No matter how clear street numbers are, if they are not earnestly implemented and there is no unified system, the result will be a failure. Other street numbers will inevitably be affected, rendering them ineffective.

According to the report, the materials used to make street signs should also be carefully selected. Wrought iron sheets, steel plates, or wood can be used, coated with enamel for aesthetics, durability, and reasonable cost.

All street numbers must be bright day and night. At night, they should be illuminated with lights. There are many methods, but the most effective is to suspend electric lights in front of the street signs or use transparent and reflective lights, depending on local conditions.

World Automobile News 233

— Pingping

The progress of automobile transportation is already very evident. According to last year's statistics, the number of registered automobiles, excluding the United States, has increased by 16.5%. Looking at the world, there are only four countries with over one million automobiles. In total, there are 32,000,000 passenger cars, freight cars, and large four-wheeled vehicles in use around the world today.

The Solomon Islands in the South Pacific has the least developed automobile industry, with only two vehicles to date. Excluding the United States, New Zealand has the most, with approximately one vehicle for every 8.9 people. Canada is third, with one vehicle for every nine people.

The United States has 77% of the world's motorized vehicles and produces 83.5% of them. Last year, European automobile companies also produced 600,000 automobiles and freight cars.

Five Countries Investigate Road Administration

The improvement of automobile transportation around the world is also evidenced by the recent fact that five representatives of leading enterprises have visited the United States to investigate best practices in road construction and passenger vehicle development.

Dr. Ang Labian, an employee of the Philippine Luzon Railway Company, is studying the operation of long-distance buses and the state of train and automobile traffic in the United States.

Mr. O'Huait of Germany is investigating American automobile tax rates and road finance as a reference for improving German taxation and road systems. Austrian road official William Bock is currently seeking evidence of all concrete and effective road construction methods. Two directors and

Page 30

Two engineers from the Tokyo Ishikawa Company of Japan are also carefully examining the use of Ishikawa's method of automobile adoption in the United States.

The former president of the Finnish Automobile Brokers Association and current transportation advisor to the Finnish Ministry, Mr. Chugola, reported to his countrymen the importance of road construction and requested the government to raise foreign debt to rectify all roads and complete unfinished projects. He is currently investigating road administration in the United States.

Extensive Road Construction in South America

"South American countries are sparing no effort in road construction. Although financial management and academics are all important issues, the government's efforts to build more roads to benefit the people are indeed urgent." This is a statement made by Mr. Aiqumi, the vice president of a regular automobile company, representing the United States at the Second Pan-American Road Congress in Rio de Janeiro. Mr. Mi also reported that while traveling, he could casually see groups of automobiles speeding along the roads. He recalled that four years ago, it was difficult to travel outside the city due to inconvenient transportation, but now it is very different, and even deep in the mountains and forests, it is possible to drive.

Points of View Vary with Time

When President Theodore Roosevelt of the United States inspected Michigan in 1909, someone described the usefulness of automobiles and requested a test ride, but his guards considered this to be a risky move and advised against it.

"An accident may occur, and the horses pulling the carriages will be frightened by an automobile."

For this reason, President Roosevelt initially hesitated before taking a seat in the automobile.

China Strives for Construction

Today, China has over 30,000 miles of roads that can be used by automobiles, and another 3,764 miles are under construction. Muddy ground is also gradually being paved into roads. Of the eighteen provinces, Guangdong and Henan have the most paved roads. The automobile route between Shanghai and Beijing is increasing daily, as is the case in central China and Zhejiang Province.

In 1922, Fujian Province had only three automobiles. Today, the province has 300 buses operating on perfectly constructed roads. The first highway between Fuzhou and the port city of Xiamen can be considered one of the most modern in the country. As for the construction of inland roads, it is proceeding without rest.

New Roads in Spain

Spain is currently constructing a modern, high-speed road from Madrid to San Pei Xing, with a length of 260 miles and a width of approximately 36 feet (6 feet for parking). The turns are wider to accommodate the accumulation of goods. When other roads meet this road, they will be higher or slightly lower. In recent years, Spain's roads have progressed rapidly. The highway between Baus Luna and the French border has now been completed, making transportation for travelers extremely convenient. Other roads, although not as wide or good as the new roads, are also flat and beautiful.

Chilean Expert Goes to Peru

The President of Chile sent a letter to the President of Peru, stating that three automobile experts from his country have recently arrived in Lima to complete the automobile highway between Santiago (Chile) and Lima (Peru). The distance between the two cities is 1,800 miles, and once the road is completed, the destination can be reached in 35 days.

Service Becomes Increasingly Widespread

One thing can prove that automobile service is ubiquitous and widespread. When travelers were traveling through the high mountains of Norway, the support of their Chevrolet car broke. They made a long-distance phone call to the town of Lali Hammer, only three thousand people, one hundred miles away. Informing the local automobile manager to come and repair it. The next day, a technician and an assistant did come. Finally, the brake was installed and restored to its original state. Four hours later, its Class A was able to resume its joyful life.

Hungary Pays Attention to First Aid

The automobile industry in Hungary is becoming increasingly important, so the Ministry of Interior has notified all drivers across the country that whenever an accident occurs, they must stop their vehicles and do their best to provide rescue. After the decree was issued, the Royal Automobile Club hired famous doctors to teach its members various first aid and treatment methods.

Hungarian reports said that the country's automobile companies have organized a mutual aid society. They help each other in times of danger. The government praised it. According to a recent survey, there are 18,741 automobiles in the country.

South African Summit Through Traffic

We have seen many times that cars can drive on the top of the mountain. Twenty-five years ago, someone investigated the total number of vehicles in the world. In South Africa, it was found that the Cape Corner was suitable for paving roads for vehicles. It is currently under construction and will be completed soon.

Drivers Like to Doze Off

An American automobile exporting company said that there is a very strange resistance to overseas sales. That is, coachmen are reluctant to switch to driving cars. When driving a car, there is no good chance to take a nap. In other words, when the coachman is pulling a heavy load, he can sit high and sleep peacefully.

French Ban Private Transfers

For example, a product produced in November 1929, claimed to be a 1930 automobile, was treated as a false statement in the Paris court and was subject to punishment. In short, when selling a car, private transfer dates are not allowed. The date of departure of the goods from the factory is the statutory standard time.

Familiar Things Hidden

The German Postal Service Headquarters issued an order to various places to improve management. From August 1, the practice of postmen walking door-to-door to deliver letters in the countryside of Hesse Province has become a thing of the past. Specially made new cars are equipped with four seats, a room for storing letters). Special service for rural delivery is required. Passenger cars have also been increased from once a day to twice a day.

Four Hundred Vehicles in Two Hours

In 1909, the average monthly production of automobile factories was at most 400 vehicles. However, progress was very rapid. Today, the Oakland Bonxia Factory can manufacture that number (400 vehicles) every two hours.

Miscellaneous Records

Buick's African Hunting Expedition 234

— Ji

Using automobiles for hunting in the African wilderness is truly an unprecedented feat. This year, an American named Beidu Shi, full of whimsy, hired twenty people. He personally drove a Buick touring car, along with six additional freight trucks. They departed from French Touraine in Africa, passing through Kaiye—Sudan—Equatorial Central Africa—Nagaria—the Sahara Desert—and finally arriving in Morocco. The journey spanned 9,500 miles and lasted five months. Mr. Bei's Buick performed exceptionally well during this long journey, with very satisfactory results. This hunting trip yielded several lions and numerous hippos, all rare and valuable trophies. The route passed through deep forests and vast swamps, and the car's speed varied depending on the road surface. It reached speeds of up to forty miles per hour at times, and as slow as ten miles per hour at others. When encountering rivers and swamps, they used canoes to ferry the vehicles across. The hunting areas were all places untouched by white men. Africa's tropical climate was overwhelmingly hot, so Mr. Bei adjusted his activities to the nighttime. One day, while dining under the trees, wild bees detected the aroma and arrived in swarms of thousands, causing great distress to Mr. Bei. Fortunately, he cut open a lemon to lure the bees away. His companions were all afraid of the bees, but Mr. Bei alone, wearing gloves and a mask, moved among them. Later, upon reaching a central station, they encountered a sleeping sickness epidemic. Mr. Bei and his party took quinine and were spared. Later, upon arriving in French Africa, they encountered road construction, which allowed for relatively fast travel. In Nagaria, Africa, there was a highway six hundred miles long, as flat and straight as could be. The car traveled on this road with unparalleled comfort. But soon the joy ended and hardship began, as crossing Nagaria meant entering the Sahara Desert. The desert was filled with yellow sand, and the road was long and arduous. In the past, driving cars in the desert required special caterpillar track attachments. Mr. Bei, trusting in the Buick's resilience, prepared 700 liters of fresh water in advance. He drove day and night. The desert is unpredictable, shifting with the wind. Driving a car through it was even more difficult than groping through a deep forest in the dark. Thankfully, the desert was quite cool at night, and the car advanced by the light of its headlights, finally reaching the plains. Mr. Bei's journey can be described as a groundbreaking achievement, traversing Africa from west to east. The Buick deserves the greatest credit. Along the way, there was only one minor repair. Afterward, Mr. Bei's Buick, after being washed, looked as good as new.
